<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Swahili language</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
				<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>				<div id="contentSub"></div>
				<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
				<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="above" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:130%; color: black; background-color:  #ffddaa;">Swahili</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:110%; color: black; background-color:  #ffddaa;">
Kiswahili</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;">Native&#160;to</th><td style="line-height:1.35em;">
<a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> (on the <a href="/wiki/Bajuni_Islands" title="Bajuni Islands">Bajuni islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barawa" title="Barawa">Barawa</a>),  <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Mwani_language" title="Mwani language">Mwani</a>), <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayotte" title="Mayotte">Mayotte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Native speakers</div></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;">
unclear. Estimates range from 2 million (2003)<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> to 15 million&#160;(2012)<sup id="cite_ref-e21_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e21-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><br />L2 speakers:90 million (1991–2015)<sup id="cite_ref-e21_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e21-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">Language family</a></span></div></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;">
<div style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger–Congo</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:1.35em;padding-left:0"><li>
<a href="/wiki/Atlantic%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Atlantic–Congo languages">Atlantic–Congo</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Benue%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Benue–Congo languages">Benue–Congo</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Southern_Bantoid_languages" title="Southern Bantoid languages">Southern Bantoid</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Northeast_Coast_Bantu_languages" title="Northeast Coast Bantu languages">Northeast Coast Bantu</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Sabaki_languages" title="Sabaki languages">Sabaki</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><b>Swahili</b></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">Writing system</a></span></div></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;">
<div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> (<a href="#Orthography">Roman Swahili alphabet</a>)</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Swahili_Braille" class="mw-redirect" title="Swahili Braille">Swahili Braille</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;color: black; background-color:  #ffddaa;">Official status</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Official language&#160;in</div></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;">

<code><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/swa" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:swa">swa</a></code> – <a href="/wiki/ISO_639_macrolanguage" title="ISO 639 macrolanguage">inclusive code</a><br />Individual codes:<br /><code><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/swc" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:swc">swc</a></code>&#160;–&#160;Congo Swahili<br /><code><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/swh" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:swh">swh</a></code>&#160;–&#160;Coastal Swahili<br /><code><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/ymk" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:ymk">ymk</a></code>&#160;–&#160;<a href="/wiki/Makwe_language" title="Makwe language">Makwe</a><br /><code><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/wmw" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:wmw">wmw</a></code>&#160;–&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mwani_language" title="Mwani language">Mwani</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em; padding-right:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></th><td style="line-height:1.35em;">

<code>99-AUS-m</code></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height:1.35em;">

<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #1A841A; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;official or national language</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #5CD65C; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;as a trade language</div></div>
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="noprint selfref" style="text-align:center;background-color:#ddddff;padding:0.3em 0.5em;text-align:left;line-height:1.3;">
<b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Replacement_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement character">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For a guide to IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Swahili</b>, also known as <b><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">Kiswahili</i></b> (translation: language of the Swahili people), is a <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu language</a> and the <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili people</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region and other parts of eastern and south-eastern Africa, including <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> (DRC).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Comorian_language" title="Comorian language">Comorian</a>, spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Comoros" title="Comoros">Comoros Islands</a> is sometimes considered to be a dialect of Swahili, though other authorities consider it a distinct language.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The exact number of Swahili speakers, be it native or second-language speakers, is unknown and a matter of debate. Various estimates have been put forward and they vary widely, from 50 million to over 100 million.<sup id="cite_ref-standford_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-standford-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Swahili serves as a <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">national language</a> of four nations: Tanzania, Kenya, Uganda, and the DRC. <a href="/wiki/Comorian_language" title="Comorian language">Shikomor</a>, the official language in Comoros and also spoken in <a href="/wiki/Mayotte" title="Mayotte">Mayotte</a> (<i lang="swb" title="Maore Comorian language text"><a href="/wiki/Shimaore" class="mw-redirect" title="Shimaore">Shimaore</a></i>), is related to Swahili.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Swahili is also one of the working <a href="/wiki/African_Union#Languages" title="African Union">languages of the African Union</a> and officially recognised as a lingua franca of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> South Africa legalized the teaching of Swahili in South African schools as an optional subject beginning in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A significant fraction of Swahili vocabulary derives from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>, in part conveyed by <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>-speaking Muslim inhabitants. For example, the Swahili word for "book" is <i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">kitabu</i>, traceable back to the Arabic word <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" title="Arabic language text">كتاب</span> <i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic language text">kitābu</i> (from the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">root</a> <i><a href="/wiki/K-T-B" title="K-T-B">k-t-b</a></i> "write"). However, the Swahili plural form of this word ("books") is <i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">vitabu</i>, following Bantu grammar in which <i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">ki-</i> is reanalysed as a nominal class prefix, whose plural is <i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">vi-</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Swahili is a <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sabaki_languages" title="Sabaki languages">Sabaki branch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Guthrie_classification_of_Bantu_languages" title="Guthrie classification of Bantu languages">Guthrie's geographic classification</a>, Swahili is in Bantu zone G, whereas the other Sabaki languages are in zone E70, commonly under the name <i>Nyika.</i> Local folk-theories of the language have often considered Swahili to be a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_language" title="Mixed language">mixed language</a> because of its many loan words from <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, and the fact that Swahili people have historically been Muslims. However, historical linguists do not consider the Arabic influence on Swahili to be significant enough to classify it as a mixed language, since Arabic influence is limited to lexical items, most of which have only been borrowed after 1500, while the grammatical and syntactic structure of the language is typically Bantu.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png/260px-Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png" width="260" height="445" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png/390px-Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png/520px-Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png 2x" data-file-width="1006" data-file-height="1723" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Askari_makumbusho_matini_Kiswahili_-_Kiarabu.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swahili in Persian script—memorial plate at the <a href="/wiki/Askari_Monument" title="Askari Monument">Askari Monument</a>, Dar es Salaam (1927)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Its old name was Kingozi, but as traders came from Arab countries, their vocabulary intermingled with the language. It was originally written in <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known documents written in Swahili are letters written in <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa</a> in 1711 in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a> that were sent to the Portuguese of Mozambique and their local allies. The original letters are preserved in the Historical Archives of Goa, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_period">Colonial period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Colonial period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Swahili-pn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Swahili-pn.jpg/260px-Swahili-pn.jpg" width="260" height="362" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/Swahili-pn.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="329" data-file-height="458" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Swahili-pn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Although originally written with the Arabic script, Swahili is now written in a <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin alphabet</a> introduced by Christian <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionaries</a> and colonial administrators. The text shown here is the Catholic version of the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord&#39;s Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Since Swahili was the language of commerce in East Africa, the colonial administrators wanted to standardize it.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In June 1928, an interterritorial conference attended by representatives of Kenya, Tanganyika, Uganda, and <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> took place in Mombasa. The Zanzibar dialect was chosen as standard Swahili for those areas,<sup id="cite_ref-:0_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> and the standard orthography for Swahili was adopted.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Current status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Swahili has become a second language spoken by tens of millions in three African Great Lakes countries (Tanzania, Kenya, and the DRC) where it is an official or national language. It is the only African language in the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>. In 2016, Swahili was made a compulsory subject in all Kenyan schools.<sup id="cite_ref-laban_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-laban-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Swahili and closely related languages are spoken by relatively small numbers of people in Burundi, Comoros, Malawi, Mozambique, Uganda, Zambia and Rwanda.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The language was still understood in the southern ports of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Kharusi_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kharusi-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some 80 percent of approximately 49 million Tanzanians speak Swahili in addition to their first languages.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The five eastern provinces of the DRC are Swahili-speaking. Nearly half the 66 million Congolese reportedly speak it.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Swahili speakers may number 120 to 150 million in total.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Unlike the vast majority of Niger-Congo languages,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Swahili lacks contrastive tone. That and the language's <a href="/wiki/Orthographic_depth" title="Orthographic depth">shallow orthography</a> make Swahili said to be the easiest African language for an English speaker to learn.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Standard Swahili has five vowel <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a>: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑ/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɛ/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔ/</span>, and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/u/</span>.  Vowels are never <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction" title="Vowel reduction">reduced</a>, regardless of <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stress</a>, but they are pronounced in full as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-:1_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɑ/</span> is pronounced like the "a" in <i>f<b>a</b>ther</i>.</li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɛ/</span> is pronounced like the "e" in <i>g<b>e</b>t</i>.</li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i/</span> is pronounced like the "ee" in <i>s<b>ee</b></i>.</li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɔ/</span> is pronounced somewhat like the "o" in <i>f<b>o</b>rd</i>.</li>
<li><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/u/</span> is pronounced like the "u" in <i>z<b>u</b>l<b>u</b> or "oo" in </i>l<b>oo</b>p<i>.</i></li></ul>

<p><br />
Swahili also has the phonemes <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pʰ tʰ tʃʰ kʰ bʰ dʰ dʒʰ ɡʰ/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> "[I]n some Arabic loans (nouns, verbs, adjectives) emphasis or intensity is expressed by reproducing the original emphatic consonants <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/dˤ, sˤ, tˤ, zˤ/</span> and the uvular <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/q/</span>, or lengthening a vowel, where aspiration would be used in inherited Bantu words."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075,_Deutsch-Ostafrika,_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg/190px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg" width="190" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg/285px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg/380px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="622" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA0075,_Deutsch-Ostafrika,_Einheimisches_M%C3%A4dchen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swahili in Arabic script on the clothes of a woman in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> (ca. early 1900s)</div></div></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(January 2016)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Swahili is currently written in an alphabet close to <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, except it does not use the letters Q and X.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>  There are two <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">digraphs</a> for native sounds, <i>ch</i> and <i>sh</i>; <i>c</i> is not used apart from unassimilated English loans and, occasionally, as a substitute for <i>k</i> in advertisements. There are also several digraphs for Arabic sounds not distinguished in pronunciation outside of traditional Swahili areas.
</p><p>The language used to be written in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>. Unlike adaptations of the Arabic script for other languages, relatively little accommodation was made for Swahili. There were also differences in orthographic conventions between cities and authors and over the centuries, some quite precise but others different enough to cause difficulties with intelligibility.
</p><p><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/e/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/o/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/u/</span> were often conflated, but in some spellings, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/e/</span> was distinguished from <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i/</span> by rotating the <i><a href="/wiki/Kasrah" class="mw-redirect" title="Kasrah">kasra</a></i> 90° and  <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/o/</span> was distinguished from  <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/u/</span> by writing the <i><a href="/wiki/Damma" class="mw-redirect" title="Damma">damma</a></i> backwards.
</p><p>Several Swahili consonants do not have equivalents in Arabic, and for them, often no special letters were created unlike, for example, <a href="/wiki/Urdu_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Urdu script">Urdu</a> script. Instead, the closest Arabic sound is substituted. Not only did that mean that one letter often stands for more than one sound, but also writers made different choices of which consonant to substitute. Here are some of the equivalents between Arabic Swahili and Roman Swahili:
</p>

<td><big><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/%D9%86" class="mw-redirect" title="ن">ن</a></span>&#x202c;</big></big>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><big><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">ـه</span>&#x202c;</big></big>

<td><big><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">ـي</span>&#x202c;</big></big>

<td><big><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">ـيـ</span>&#x202c;</big></big>
</td>
<td><big><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">يـ</span>&#x202c;</big></big>
</td>
<td><big><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/%D9%8A" class="mw-redirect" title="ي">ي</a></span>&#x202c;</big></big>
</td>
<td>y ny
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>That was the general situation, but conventions from Urdu were adopted by some authors so as to distinguish aspiration and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span> from <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/b/</span>: <span lang="sw-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Swahili language text">پھا</span>&#8206; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/pʰaa/</span> 'gazelle', <span lang="sw-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Swahili language text">پا</span>&#8206; <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/paa/</span> 'roof'. Although it is not found in Standard Swahili today, there is a distinction between dental and <a href="/wiki/Alveolar_consonant" title="Alveolar consonant">alveolar consonants</a> in some dialects, which is reflected in some orthographies, for example in <span lang="sw-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Swahili language text">كُٹَ</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">-kuta</i></i> 'to meet' vs. <span lang="sw-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Swahili language text">كُتَ</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">-kut̠a</i></i> 'to be satisfied'. A <i>k</i> with the dots of <i>y</i>, <span style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.5em 0.25em 0.25em; border-right: none; width: 1em;"><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">ﮛ</span>&#x202c;</big></span><span style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.5em 0.25em 0.25em; border-right: none; width: 1em;"><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">ﮝ</span>&#x202c;</big></span><span style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.5em 0.25em 0.25em; border-right: none; width: 1em;"><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">ﮜ</span>&#x202c;</big></span><span style="border: 1px solid #a2a9b1; background: #f8f9fa; padding: 0.5em 0.25em 0.25em; width: 1em;"><big><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,&#39;Noto Naskh Arabic&#39;,&#39;Droid Arabic Naskh&#39;,Harmattan,&#39;Arabic Typesetting&#39;,&#39;Traditional Arabic&#39;,&#39;Simplified Arabic&#39;,&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,Arial,&#39;Sakkal Majalla&#39;,&#39;Microsoft Uighur&#39;,Calibri,&#39;Microsoft Sans Serif&#39;,&#39;Segoe UI&#39;,serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">ﮚ</span>&#x202c;</big></span>, was used for <i>ch</i> in some conventions; <i>ky</i> being historically and even contemporaneously a more accurate transcription than Roman <i>ch</i>. In Mombasa, it was common to use the Arabic emphatics for Cw, for example in <span lang="sw-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Swahili language text">صِصِ</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">swiswi</i></i> (standard <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">sisi</i></i>) 'we' and <span lang="sw-Arab" dir="rtl" title="Swahili language text">كِطَ</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">kit̠wa</i></i> (standard <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">kichwa</i></i>) 'head'.
</p><p>Word division differs from Roman norms. Particles such as <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">ya, na, si, kwa, ni</i></i> are joined to the following noun, and possessives such as <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">yangu</i></i> and <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">yako</i></i> are joined to the preceding noun, but verbs are written as two words, with the subject and <a href="/wiki/Tense%E2%80%93aspect%E2%80%93mood" title="Tense–aspect–mood">tense–aspect–mood</a> morphemes separated from the object and root, as in <i><i lang="sw" title="Swahili language text">aliye niambia</i></i> "he who told me".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Swahili_grammar" title="Swahili grammar">Swahili grammar</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noun_classes">Noun classes <span id="Noun_classes"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Noun classes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Semantic_motivation">Semantic motivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Semantic motivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(January 2016)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <i>ki-/vi-</i> class historically consisted of two separate genders, artefacts (Bantu class 7/8, utensils and hand tools mostly) and diminutives (Bantu class 12), which were conflated at a stage ancestral to Swahili. Examples of the former are <i>kisu</i> "knife", <i>kiti</i> "chair" (from <i>mti</i> "tree, wood"), <i>chombo</i> "vessel" (a contraction of <i>ki-ombo</i>). Examples of the latter are <i>kitoto</i> "infant", from <i>mtoto</i> "child"; <i>kitawi</i> "frond", from <i>tawi</i> "branch"; and <i>chumba</i> (<i>ki-umba</i>) "room", from <i>nyumba</i> "house". It is the diminutive sense that has been furthest extended. An extension common to diminutives in many languages is <i>approximation</i> and <i>resemblance</i> (having a 'little bit' of some characteristic, like <i>-y</i> or <i>-ish</i> in English). For example, there is <i>kijani</i> "green", from <i>jani</i> "leaf" (compare English 'leafy'), <i>kichaka</i> "bush" from <i>chaka</i> "clump", and <i>kivuli</i> "shadow" from <i>uvuli</i> "shade". A 'little bit' of a verb would be an instance of an action, and such <i>instantiations</i> (usually not very active ones) are found: <i>kifo</i> "death", from the verb <i>-fa</i> "to die"; <i>kiota</i> "nest" from <i>-ota</i> "to brood"; <i>chakula</i> "food" from <i>kula</i> "to eat"; <i>kivuko</i> "a ford, a pass" from <i>-vuka</i> "to cross"; and <i>kilimia</i> "the <a href="/wiki/Pleiades" title="Pleiades">Pleiades</a>", from <i>-limia</i> "to farm with", from its role in guiding planting. A resemblance, or being a bit like something, implies marginal status in a category, so things that are marginal examples of their class may take the <i>ki-/vi-</i> prefixes. One example is <i>chura</i> (<i>ki-ura</i>) "frog", which is only half terrestrial and therefore is marginal as an animal. This extension may account for disabilities as well: <i>kilema</i> "a cripple", <i>kipofu</i> "a blind person", <i>kiziwi</i> "a deaf person". Finally, diminutives often denote contempt, and contempt is sometimes expressed against things that are dangerous. This might be the historical explanation for <i>kifaru</i> "rhinoceros", <i>kingugwa</i> "spotted hyena", and <i>kiboko</i> "hippopotamus" (perhaps originally meaning "stubby legs").
</p><p>Another class with broad semantic extension is the <i>m-/mi-</i> class (Bantu classes 3/4). This is often called the 'tree' class, because <i>mti, miti</i> "tree(s)" is the prototypical example. However, it seems to cover vital entities neither human nor typical animals: trees and other plants, such as <i>mwitu</i> 'forest' and <i>mtama</i> 'millet' (and from there, things made from plants, like <i>mkeka</i> 'mat'); supernatural and natural forces, such as <i>mwezi</i> 'moon', <i>mlima</i> 'mountain', <i>mto</i> 'river'; active things, such as <i>moto</i> 'fire', including active body parts (<i>moyo</i> 'heart', <i>mkono</i> 'hand, arm'); and human groups, which are vital but not themselves human, such as <i>mji</i> 'village', and, by analogy, <i>mzinga</i> 'beehive/cannon'. From the central idea of <i>tree</i>, which is thin, tall, and spreading, comes an extension to other long or extended things or parts of things, such as <i>mwavuli</i> 'umbrella', <i>moshi</i> 'smoke', <i>msumari</i> 'nail'; and from activity there even come active instantiations of verbs, such as <i>mfuo</i> "metal forging", from <i>-fua</i> "to forge", or <i>mlio</i> "a sound", from <i>-lia</i> "to make a sound". Words may be connected to their class by more than one metaphor. For example, <i>mkono</i> is an active body part, and <i>mto</i> is an active natural force, but they are also both long and thin. Things with a trajectory, such as <i>mpaka</i> 'border' and <i>mwendo</i> 'journey', are classified with long thin things, as in many other languages with noun classes. This may be further extended to anything dealing with time, such as <i>mwaka</i> 'year' and perhaps <i>mshahara</i> 'wages'. Animals exceptional in some way and so not easily fitting in the other classes may be placed in this class.
</p><p>The other classes have foundations that may at first seem similarly counterintuitive.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In short,
</p>
<ul><li>Classes 1–2 include most words for people: kin terms, professions, ethnicities, etc., including translations of most English words ending in <i>-er.</i> They include a couple generic words for animals: <i>mnyama</i> 'beast', <i>mdudu</i> 'bug'.</li>
<li>Classes 5–6 have a broad semantic range of groups, expanses, and augmentatives. Although interrelated, it is easier to illustrate if broken down:
<ul><li>Augmentatives, such as <i>joka</i> 'serpent' from <i>nyoka</i> 'snake', lead to titles and other terms of respect (the opposite of diminutives, which lead to terms of contempt): <i>Bwana</i> 'Sir', <i>shangazi</i> 'aunt', <i>fundi</i> 'craftsman', <i>kadhi</i> 'judge'.</li>
<li>Expanses: <i>ziwa</i> 'lake', <i>bonde</i> 'valley', <i>taifa</i> 'country', <i>anga</i> 'sky'
<ul><li>from this, mass nouns: <i>maji</i> 'water', <i>vumbi</i> 'dust' (and other liquids and fine particulates which may cover broad expanses), <i>kaa</i> 'charcoal', <i>mali</i> 'wealth', <i>maridhawa</i> 'abundance'</li></ul></li>
<li>Collectives: <i>kundi</i> 'group', <i>kabila</i> 'language/ethnic group', <i>jeshi</i> 'army', <i>daraja</i> ' stairs', <i>manyoya</i> 'fur, feathers', <i>mapesa</i> 'small change', <i>manyasi</i> 'weeds', <i>jongoo</i> 'millipede' (large set of legs), <i>marimba</i> 'xylophone' (large set of keys)
<ul><li>from this, individual things found in groups: <i>jiwe</i> 'stone', <i>tawi</i> 'branch', <i>ua</i> 'flower', <i>tunda</i> 'fruit' (also the names of most fruits), <i>yai</i> 'egg', <i>mapacha</i> 'twins', <i>jino</i> 'tooth', <i>tumbo</i> 'stomach' (cf. English "guts"), and paired body parts such as <i>jicho</i> 'eye', <i>bawa</i> 'wing', etc.</li>
<li>also collective or dialogic actions, which occur among groups of people: <i>neno</i> 'a word', from <i>kunena</i> 'to speak' (and by extension, mental verbal processes: <i>wazo</i> 'thought', <i>maana</i> 'meaning'); <i>pigo</i> 'a stroke, blow', from <i>kupiga</i> 'to hit'; <i>gomvi</i> 'a quarrel', <i>shauri</i> 'advice, plan', <i>kosa</i> 'mistake', <i>jambo</i> 'affair', <i>penzi</i> 'love', <i>jibu</i> 'answer', <i>agano</i> 'promise', <i>malipo</i> 'payment'</li>
<li>From pairing, reproduction is suggested as another extension (fruit, egg, testicle, flower, twins, etc.), but these generally duplicate one or more of the subcategories above</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>Classes 9–10 are used for most typical animals: <i>ndege</i> 'bird', <i>samaki</i> 'fish', and the specific names of typical beasts, birds, and bugs. However, this is the 'other' class, for words not fitting well elsewhere, and about half of the class 9–10 nouns are foreign loanwords. Loans may be classified as 9–10 because they lack the prefixes inherent in other classes, and most native class 9–10 nouns have no prefix. Thus they do not form a coherent semantic class, though there are still semantic extensions from individual words.</li>
<li>Class 11 (which takes class 10 for the plural) are mostly nouns with an "extended outline shape", in either one dimension or two:
<ul><li>mass nouns that are generally localized rather than covering vast expanses: <i>uji</i> 'porridge', <i>wali</i> 'cooked rice'</li>
<li>broad: <i>ukuta</i> 'wall', <i>ukucha</i> 'fingernail', <i>upande</i> 'side' (≈ <i>ubavu</i> 'rib'), <i>wavu</i> 'net', <i>wayo</i> 'sole, footprint', <i>ua</i> 'fence, yard', <i>uteo</i> 'winnowing basket',</li>
<li>long: <i>utambi</i> 'wick', <i>utepe</i> 'stripe', <i>uta</i> 'bow', <i>ubavu</i> 'rib', <i>ufa</i> 'crack', <i>unywele</i> 'a hair'
<ul><li>from 'a hair', <a href="/wiki/Singulative" class="mw-redirect" title="Singulative">singulatives</a> of nouns, which are often class 6 ('collectives') in the plural: <i>unyoya</i> 'a feather', <i>uvumbi</i> 'a grain of dust', <i>ushanga</i> 'a bead'</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>Class 14 are abstractions, such as <i>utoto</i> 'childhood' (from <i>mtoto</i> 'a child') and have no plural. They have the same prefixes and concord as class 11, except optionally for adjectival concord.</li>
<li>Class 15 are verbal infinitives.</li>
<li>Classes 16–18 are locatives. The Bantu nouns of these classes have been lost; the only permanent member is the Arabic loan <i>mahali</i> 'place(s)', but  in Mombasa Swahili, the old prefixes survive: <i>pahali</i> 'place', <i>mwahali</i> 'places'. However, any noun with the locative suffix <i>-ni</i> takes class 16–18 agreement. The distinction between them is that class 16 agreement is used if the location is intended to be definite ("at"), class 17 if indefinite ("around") or involves motion ("to, toward"), and class 18 if it involves containment ("within"): <i>mahali pazuri</i> 'a good spot', <i>mahali kuzuri</i> 'a nice area', <i>mahali muzuri</i> (it's nice in there).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agreement">Agreement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Agreement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(January 2016)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Swahili phrases agree with nouns in a system of <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">concord</a>, but if the noun refers to a human, they accord with noun classes 1-2 regardless of their noun class. Verbs agree with the noun class of their subjects and objects; adjectives, prepositions and demonstratives agree with the noun class of their nouns. In Standard Swahili <i>(Kiswahili sanifu)</i>, based on the dialect spoken in Zanzibar, the system is rather complex; however, it is drastically simplified in many local variants where Swahili is not a native language, such as in Nairobi. In non-native Swahili, concord reflects only animacy: human subjects and objects trigger <i>a-, wa-</i> and <i>m-, wa-</i> in verbal concord, while non-human subjects and objects of whatever class trigger <i>i-, zi-</i>. Infinitives vary between standard <i>ku-</i> and reduced <i>i-.</i><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> ("Of" is animate <i>wa</i> and inanimate <i>ya, za.</i>)
</p><p>In Standard Swahili, human subjects and objects of whatever class trigger animacy concord in <i>a-, wa-</i> and <i>m-, wa-,</i> and non-human subjects and objects trigger a variety of gender-concord prefixes.
</p>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most Swahili adjectives begin with either a consonant or the vowels <i>i-</i> or <i>e-</i>, listed separately above. The few adjectives beginning with other vowels do not agree with all noun classes since some are restricted to humans. NC 1 <i>m(w)-</i> is <i>mw-</i> before <i>a</i> and <i>o,</i> and reduces to <i>m-</i> before <i>u</i>; <i>wa-</i> does not change; and <i>ki-, vi-, mi-</i> become <i>ch-, vy-, my-</i> before <i>o</i> but not before <i>u</i>: <i>mwanana, waanana</i> "gentle", <i>mwororo, waororo, myororo, chororo, vyororo</i> "mild, yielding", <i>mume, waume, kiume, viume</i> "male".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a few verbs: <i>kwenda, kwisha</i></span>
</li>
</ol></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects_and_closely_related_languages">Dialects and closely related languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Dialects and closely related languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>This list is based on <i>Swahili and Sabaki: a linguistic history</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern standard Swahili is based on <i>Kiunguja,</i> the dialect spoken in <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar_Town" class="mw-redirect" title="Zanzibar Town">Zanzibar Town</a>, but there are numerous dialects of Swahili, some of which are mutually unintelligible, such as the following:<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_dialects">Old dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Old dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Maho (2009) considers these to be distinct languages:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Kimwani" class="mw-redirect" title="Kimwani">Kimwani</a></i> is spoken in the Kerimba Islands and northern coastal Mozambique.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Bravanese_dialect" title="Bravanese dialect">Chimwiini</a></i> is spoken by the ethnic minorities in and around the town of <a href="/wiki/Barawa" title="Barawa">Barawa</a> on the southern coast of Somalia.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kibajuni_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Kibajuni dialect">Kibajuni</a></i> is spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Bajuni_people" title="Bajuni people">Bajuni</a> minority ethnic group on the coast and islands on both sides of the Somali–Kenyan border and in the <a href="/wiki/Bajuni_Islands" title="Bajuni Islands">Bajuni Islands</a> (the northern part of the Lamu archipelago) and is also called <i>Kitikuu</i> and <i>Kigunya</i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Socotra_Swahili" class="mw-redirect" title="Socotra Swahili">Socotra Swahili</a> (extinct)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sidi_language" title="Sidi language">Sidi</a>, in Gujarat (extinct)</li></ul>
<p>The rest of the dialects are divided by him into two groups:
</p>
<ul><li>Mombasa–Lamu Swahili
<ul><li>Lamu
<ul><li><i>Kiamu</i> is spoken in and around the island of <a href="/wiki/Lamu" title="Lamu">Lamu</a> (Amu).</li>
<li><i>Kipate</i> is a local dialect of <a href="/wiki/Pate_Island" title="Pate Island">Pate Island</a>, considered to be closest to the original dialect of Kingozi.</li>
<li><i>Kingozi</i> is an ancient dialect spoken on the Indian Ocean coast between Lamu and Somalia and is sometimes still used in poetry. It is often considered the source of Swahili.</li></ul></li>
<li>Mombasa
<ul><li><i>Chijomvu</i> is a subdialect of the Mombasa area.</li>
<li><i>Kimvita</i> is the major dialect of Mombasa (also known as "Mvita", which means "war", in reference to the many wars which were fought over it), the other major dialect alongside Kiunguja.</li>
<li><i>Kingare</i> is the subdialect of the Mombasa area.</li></ul></li>
<li><i>Kimrima</i> is spoken around <a href="/wiki/Pangani" title="Pangani">Pangani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanga" title="Vanga">Vanga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dar_es_Salaam" title="Dar es Salaam">Dar es Salaam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rufiji_District" title="Rufiji District">Rufiji</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mafia_Island" title="Mafia Island">Mafia Island</a>.</li>
<li><i>Kiunguja</i> is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Stone_Town" title="Stone Town">Zanzibar City</a> and environs on <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Unguja (Zanzibar) Island</a>. Kitumbatu (Pemba) dialects occupy the bulk of the island.</li>
<li>Mambrui, Malindi</li>
<li><i>Chichifundi</i>, a dialect of the southern Kenya coast.</li>
<li>Chwaka</li>
<li><i>Kivumba</i>, a dialect of the southern Kenya coast.</li>
<li>Nosse Be (Madagascar)</li></ul></li>
<li>Pemba Swahili
<ul><li><i>Kipemba</i> is a local dialect of the <a href="/wiki/Pemba_Island" title="Pemba Island">Pemba Island</a>.</li>
<li><i>Kitumbatu</i> and <i>Kimakunduchi</i> are the countryside dialects of the island of Zanzibar. Kimakunduchi is a recent renaming of "Kihadimu"; the old name means "serf" and so is considered pejorative.</li>
<li>Makunduchi</li>
<li>Mafia, Mbwera</li>
<li>Kilwa (extinct)</li>
<li><i>Kimgao</i> used to be spoken around <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_District" title="Kilwa District">Kilwa District</a> and to the south.</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>Maho includes the various <a href="/wiki/Comorian_language" title="Comorian language">Comorian</a> dialects as a third group. Most other authorities consider Comorian to be a <a href="/wiki/Sabaki_languages" title="Sabaki languages">Sabaki</a> language, distinct from Swahili.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_regions">Other regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Other regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a> language predominates, a variant of Swahili referred to as <a href="/wiki/Bravanese_dialect" title="Bravanese dialect">Chimwiini</a> (also known as Chimbalazi) is spoken along the <a href="/wiki/Benadir" class="mw-redirect" title="Benadir">Benadir</a> coast by the <a href="/wiki/Bravanese_people" title="Bravanese people">Bravanese people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnolsom_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnolsom-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Another Swahili dialect known as <a href="/wiki/Kibajuni_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Kibajuni dialect">Kibajuni</a> also serves as the mother tongue of the <a href="/wiki/Bajuni_people" title="Bajuni people">Bajuni</a> minority ethnic group, which lives in the tiny <a href="/wiki/Bajuni_Islands" title="Bajuni Islands">Bajuni Islands</a> as well as the southern <a href="/wiki/Kismayo" title="Kismayo">Kismayo</a> region.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnolsom_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnolsom-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Godfrey_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Godfrey-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>, an estimated 22,000 people speak Swahili.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethnoloma_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnoloma-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Most are descendants of those repatriated after the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Zanzibar" title="Sultanate of Zanzibar">Sultanate of Zanzibar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-African_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-African-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Josephi_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josephi-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swahili_poets">Swahili poets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Swahili_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Swahili poets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
